{"architecture": "PARENT_MODULE_PATH: langProBe.hover.hover_pipeline.HoverMultiHopPipeline\nMETRIC_MODULE_PATH: langProBe.hover.hover_utils.discrete_retrieval_eval\n\n## Architecture Summary\n\nThis program implements a multi-hop document retrieval system for the HoVer fact-checking benchmark using DSPy. Given a claim, it performs three iterative retrieval hops to gather supporting documents needed for verification.\n\n**Key Modules:**\n- `HoverMultiHopPipeline`: Entry point wrapper that initializes ColBERTv2 retriever and delegates to the core program. Enforces max 21 documents constraint.\n- `HoverMultiHop`: Core 3-hop retrieval logic. Uses ChainOfThought modules to generate queries and summarize retrieved passages at each hop.\n- `hover_data.py`: Loads HoVer dataset, filters for 3-hop examples, and formats as DSPy examples.\n- `hover_utils.py`: Contains the `discrete_retrieval_eval` metric function.\n\n**Data Flow:**\n1. Takes a claim as input\n2. HOP 1: Retrieves k=7 documents using claim, summarizes them\n3. HOP 2: Generates new query from claim+summary_1, retrieves k=7 more docs, summarizes\n4. HOP 3: Generates query from claim+both summaries, retrieves k=7 final docs\n5. Returns all 21 retrieved documents (7 per hop)\n\n**Metric:** `discrete_retrieval_eval` evaluates whether all gold supporting document titles are present in the retrieved set (up to 21 docs), returning binary success/failure."}

Iteration 1: Proposed new text for program.create_query_hop2.predict: Given an input with two fields:

- `claim`: a statement or assertion that may contain one or multiple factual elements.
- `summary_1`: a summary evaluating the claim, indicating which parts are supported, unsupported, or partly supported/unverifiable based on the given information.

Your task is to produce a `query` field that designs precise, comprehensive search queries or information requests intended to obtain authoritative supporting evidence relevant to verifying the factual accuracy of the claim. The queries should be constructed to target reliable sources such as official documents, primary texts, reputable databases, established encyclopedias, scholarly works, or trusted media, depending on the claim’s domain.

Instructions and task details:

1. **Analyze the claim and summary_1 carefully**:  
   - Identify each discrete factual assertion or component within the claim. Claims often contain multiple parts that may be independently supported or contradicted, so treat each separately.  
   - Use the summary_1 evaluation to understand which parts are supported, contradicted, or unverifiable based on the provided information.

2. **Incorporate domain-specific knowledge and terminology**:  
   - Claims relate to factual data across many domains (e.g., video games, literature, botany, history, cinema, religion, geography, sports). Identify entities (people, places, films, artifacts, concepts) and domain-specific keywords to focus queries effectively.  
   - Include key terms such as names (e.g., Alma Wade, Rachelle Beinart), works (e.g., The Comedy of Errors, Darkened Skye), organizations (e.g., University of Texas System), events (e.g., 1980 French Open), technical terms (e.g., LithTech Jupiter EX engine), or cultural concepts (e.g., "roman à clef," "weighing of the heart").

3. **Design queries to verify all claim components explicitly**:  
   - For each unsupported or ambiguous factual element, formulate queries aimed at confirming or disproving those elements.  
   - When the claim involves relationships (e.g., "X is named after Y," "person A worked with composer B," or "film A is a remake of film B"), design queries that validate these relationships directly.  
   - Queries often involve biographical details, release dates, technical attributions, historical facts, or definitions/translations.

4. **Aim for queries that extract authoritative, precise information**:  
   - Request sources such as official credits, developer or publisher statements, scholarly articles, professional databases, encyclopedias with citations, institutional records, or direct quotes from primary texts where relevant.  
   - Where numeric or quantitative data is involved (e.g., campus acreage, enrollment numbers, dates), include terms like "official," "factbook," "statistics," or restrict searches to credible domains (`.edu`, `.gov`, established media sites).

5. **If the claim has partially supported elements, queries should focus on the unsupported or ambiguous parts**:  
   - When summary_1 states partial support, identify specifically what remains unverified or contradictory and target those aspects.  
   - Queries may clarify ambiguous terms, verify dates, places, names, or roles.

6. **When a claim includes misinformation or conflated identities, seek clarifying queries**:  
   - Check for common confusions (e.g., mixing up persons with similar names, wrong dates, or titles) by querying each identity or fact explicitly.  
   - When necessary, include queries that check for possible confusion or misattribution.

7. **Format queries as clear, focused search strings or question phrases**:  
   - Use keyword combinations, quotations for exact phrases, logical operators like OR/AND (if helpful), and site/domain restrictions to improve precision.  
   - Clearly separate multiple queries with numbering or bullet points when more than one query is needed.

8. **Include, if applicable, instructions to retrieve citations or textual excerpts for direct evidence**:  
   - For scholarly or religious claims, requests should include authoritative translations or line references.  
   - For technical or niche claims, prioritize official documentation or developer/publisher credits.

9. **Tailor queries to the nature of the claim and expected evidence type**:  
   - E.g., for historical or biographical claims, biographical databases, encyclopedias, or newspapers.  
   - For entertainment industry claims, IMDb, film credits, or reputable media sites.  
   - For scientific or botanical claims, academic journals, taxonomic databases, or field guides.  
   - For linguistic or literary claims, dictionaries, glossaries, or literary analyses.

Overall, your `query` output should serve as an efficient, authoritative search plan to conclusively support or refute each part of the claim by gathering the necessary evidence indicated as absent or unclear in the summary.

[AGENT] Change request (full): Implement a Query Decomposition Architecture in HoverMultiHopPipeline that explicitly extracts named entities from the claim and retrieves documents for each entity before performing relational queries.

SPECIFIC CHANGES:

1. Create a new DSPy Signature class `EntityExtractor` in hover_program.py that takes a claim and outputs a list of key named entities (people, places, organizations, titles) mentioned in the claim.

2. Create a new DSPy Signature class `EntityQueryGenerator` that takes an entity name and the original claim, and generates a focused retrieval query for that specific entity.

3. Modify the HoverMultiHop.forward() method to implement this new architecture:
   - Phase 1 (Entity Retrieval): Extract entities from claim using dspy.ChainOfThought(EntityExtractor), then for each entity (up to 3 entities max to respect the 3-query limit), generate a targeted query using dspy.Predict(EntityQueryGenerator) and retrieve k=7 documents per entity query
   - Phase 2 (Reranking): Collect all retrieved documents from entity queries, deduplicate them, then use a new DSPy Signature `DocumentReranker` with dspy.ChainOfThought to score and rerank all documents based on relevance to the claim, keeping only the top 21 documents
   
4. The reranker should take the claim and the list of retrieved documents as input, and output a ranked list of document indices/titles with relevance scores. Select the top 21 documents by score.

This architecture ensures that all named entities in the claim have dedicated retrieval attempts, addressing the pattern where critical entity-bridging documents are missed. The reranking phase ensures we stay within the 21-document constraint while maximizing coverage of all relevant entities.

Iteration 3: Proposed new text for program.create_query_hop3.predict: Task Description:

You will be given three text fields: `claim`, `summary_1`, and `summary_2`.

- `claim` is a factual or composite statement that asserts certain information, often consisting of multiple sub-claims or connected facts.
- `summary_1` and `summary_2` are human-generated evaluations of the claim’s accuracy based on some underlying evidence or passages (not directly provided to you). These summaries indicate whether the claim is supported, partially supported, not supported, or lacks sufficient evidence in the given passages. They may also identify specific factual points confirmed or contradicted by the sources.

Your goal is to produce a detailed and precise `query` field designed to help verify, support, or refute the claim, using the information from `claim`, `summary_1`, and `summary_2`. The `query` should be formulated as well-structured, targeted, and clear natural language search phrases or sets of key research questions that an expert researcher, fact-checker, or a search engine could use to find authoritative sources to assess the claim accurately.

---

Detailed Guidelines and Domain-Specific Considerations:

1. **Use the summaries to identify factual gaps or contradictions:**  
   - If summaries agree the claim is supported, focus the query on sourcing primary evidence for the claim’s key components (e.g., names, dates, titles, roles).  
   - If the summaries indicate partial support or contradictions, highlight which sub-claims need further verification or correction.  
   - If the claim cannot be verified due to missing information in the passages (e.g., unknown person, unclear attribution), queries should target:
     - Identification of the missing entities or details (e.g., who exactly co-wrote a work, who hosted a show, film credits, birth/death dates).  
     - Clarification of relationships or roles mentioned in the claim.

2. **Capture the multifaceted nature of many claims:**  
   Claims often contain multiple components: names, dates, roles, events, relationships, production credits, nationalities, historical contexts, etc. Queries should explicitly include all critical elements of the claim to ensure a comprehensive fact-check.

3. **Prioritize domain-relevant keywords and entities:**  
   Incorporate domain-specific terminology, such as:  
   - Film/TV: film titles, directors, actors, roles, award nominations, release years, character names.  
   - Historical/biographical: dates, lifespans, official titles, dynasties, political offices.  
   - Geography/demographics: place names, population statistics, census years.  
   - Music/literature: authors, works, collaborations, nationalities.  

4. **Leverage the reasoning demonstrated in the summaries:**  
   Use clues from summaries about ambiguity, uncertainty, or conflicts (e.g., name variants, partial evidence) to shape precise queries that can resolve those issues.

5. **Use search strategies that facilitate finding authoritative and primary sources:**  
   Include references to known reliable source types (e.g., IMDb, Wikipedia, census data, academic history sources, official filmography, official award pages) in the query formulation when applicable.

6. **Phrase queries as natural language search phrases or targeted research questions:**  
   - Queries can be multifaceted lists or bullet points, explicitly requesting specific data or confirmation, or  
   - Concise combined search phrases including key terms and filtering by known sources (e.g., `"Jack Nicholson Hoffa 1992 Danny DeVito" site:imdb.com OR site:wikipedia.org`).

---

Summary: Produce fact-checking or fact-finding queries tailored to the exact verification needs highlighted by the claim and the summaries, using detailed domain knowledge and logical analysis. Queries should be thoughtfully designed to locate missing support, clarify contradictory points, or confirm asserted facts with authoritative evidence.

Iteration 4: Proposed new text for program.create_query_hop2.predict: Given an input with two fields:

- `claim`: a statement or assertion that may contain one or multiple factual elements.
- `summary_1`: a summary evaluating the claim, indicating which parts are supported, unsupported, or partly supported/unverifiable based on the given information.

Your task is to produce a `query` field that designs precise, comprehensive search queries or information requests intended to obtain authoritative supporting evidence relevant to verifying the factual accuracy of the claim. The queries should be constructed to target reliable sources such as official documents, primary texts, reputable databases, established encyclopedias, scholarly works, or trusted media, depending on the claim’s domain.

Instructions and task details:

1. **Analyze the claim and summary_1 carefully**:  
   - Identify each discrete factual assertion or component within the claim. Claims often contain multiple parts that may be independently supported or contradicted, so treat each separately.  
   - Use the summary_1 evaluation to understand which parts are supported, contradicted, or unverifiable based on the provided information.

2. **Incorporate domain-specific knowledge and terminology**:  
   - Claims relate to factual data across many domains (e.g., video games, literature, botany, history, cinema, religion, geography, sports). Identify entities (people, places, films, artifacts, concepts) and domain-specific keywords to focus queries effectively.  
   - Include key terms such as names (e.g., Alma Wade, Rachelle Beinart), works (e.g., The Comedy of Errors, Darkened Skye), organizations (e.g., University of Texas System), events (e.g., 1980 French Open), technical terms (e.g., LithTech Jupiter EX engine), or cultural concepts (e.g., "roman à clef," "weighing of the heart").

3. **Design queries to verify all claim components explicitly**:  
   - For each unsupported or ambiguous factual element, formulate queries aimed at confirming or disproving those elements.  
   - When the claim involves relationships (e.g., "X is named after Y," "person A worked with composer B," or "film A is a remake of film B"), design queries that validate these relationships directly.  
   - Queries often involve biographical details, release dates, technical attributions, historical facts, or definitions/translations.

4. **Aim for queries that extract authoritative, precise information**:  
   - Request sources such as official credits, developer or publisher statements, scholarly articles, professional databases, encyclopedias with citations, institutional records, or direct quotes from primary texts where relevant.  
   - Where numeric or quantitative data is involved (e.g., campus acreage, enrollment numbers, dates), include terms like "official," "factbook," "statistics," or restrict searches to credible domains (`.edu`, `.gov`, established media sites).

5. **If the claim has partially supported elements, queries should focus on the unsupported or ambiguous parts**:  
   - When summary_1 states partial support, identify specifically what remains unverified or contradictory and target those aspects.  
   - Queries may clarify ambiguous terms, verify dates, places, names, or roles.

6. **When a claim includes misinformation or conflated identities, seek clarifying queries**:  
   - Check for common confusions (e.g., mixing up persons with similar names, wrong dates, or titles) by querying each identity or fact explicitly.  
   - When necessary, include queries that check for possible confusion or misattribution.

7. **Format queries as clear, focused search strings or question phrases**:  
   - Use keyword combinations, quotations for exact phrases, logical operators like OR/AND (if helpful), and site/domain restrictions to improve precision.  
   - Clearly separate multiple queries with numbering or bullet points when more than one query is needed.

8. **Include, if applicable, instructions to retrieve citations or textual excerpts for direct evidence**:  
   - For scholarly or religious claims, requests should include authoritative translations or line references.  
   - For technical or niche claims, prioritize official documentation or developer/publisher credits.

9. **Tailor queries to the nature of the claim and expected evidence type**:  
   - E.g., for historical or biographical claims, biographical databases, encyclopedias, or newspapers.  
   - For entertainment industry claims, IMDb, film credits, or reputable media sites.  
   - For scientific or botanical claims, academic journals, taxonomic databases, or field guides.  
   - For linguistic or literary claims, dictionaries, glossaries, or literary analyses.

Overall, your `query` output should serve as an efficient, authoritative search plan to conclusively support or refute each part of the claim by gathering the necessary evidence indicated as absent or unclear in the summary.

Iteration 5: Proposed new text for program.summarize1.predict: Task: Given an input containing two fields — `claim` (a factual statement or assertion) and `passages` (a list of text excerpts or documents relevant to the claim) — your goal is to produce a `summary` that evaluates the support for the claim based solely on the provided passages.

Detailed Instructions:  
1. **Understand the claim**: Carefully read the claim to identify all assertions, facts, entities, relationships, dates, numbers, and attributes mentioned. The claim may contain multiple sub-claims or comparative statements.

2. **Extract evidence from passages**:  
   - Read through all the passages meticulously to find information that either supports or refutes parts of the claim.  
   - Identify key factual details, such as names (people, places, organizations), dates, numerical data (populations, years, counts), relationships, roles, titles, outcomes, and other domain-specific elements.  
   - Note that relevant evidence may be spread across multiple passages and may require combining facts.  
   - If the passage list references multiple entities with similar names or concepts, ensure you associate facts correctly with the claim components to avoid misattribution.  

3. **Assess support status**:  
   - Determine whether the passages fully support the claim, partially support it, contradict it, or lack sufficient information.  
   - If the passages confirm some aspects of the claim but not others, categorize the overall summary as "partially supported" or "not supported" accordingly.  
   - Explicitly note any factual discrepancies or missing data points that prevent verification.

4. **Incorporate domain-specific knowledge only if present in the passages**:  
   - Avoid relying on outside or external knowledge not contained in the provided passages.  
   - For example, confirm dates, places, and titles only if present; if population figures or biographical facts are external and not in the passages, mention them as unverifiable within this context.  
   - Do not infer connections or facts without textual evidence from the passages.

5. **Compose the summary**:  
   - Summarize the verification result clearly and succinctly.  
   - Reference explicitly which portion(s) of the claim are supported, contradicted, or unverifiable by the passages.  
   - When necessary, mention that specific details (e.g., a person’s exact birthdate, an event’s location) are not found in the passages.  
   - Use phrases like "Supported," "Not supported," "Partially supported," or "Cannot verify" to classify the claim relative to the passages.

6. **Reasoning transparency (optional but recommended for quality)**:  
   - Include brief reasoning or evidence linking claim elements to specific passages or sentences, especially for complex claims involving comparisons, dates, or roles.  
   - This reasoning should clarify how the assessment was reached without adding external information.

Summary:  
You are performing a fact-checking and claim verification task using only the supplied evidence passages. Your output is a clear, concise summary that states whether the claim is supported, partially supported, refuted, or unverifiable given the input passages, explaining key evidence or lack thereof. Handle multiple claim elements carefully, and explicitly note when evidence is missing or insufficient.

Domain-Specific Notes:  
- Entities include people (actors, directors, musicians), places (airports, districts, films’ production locations), events (sports matches, film releases), and artistic works (films, operas, albums).  
- Dates and numerical information may be critical for verifying timelines, birthdates, or counts (albums, acts in an opera, population numbers).  
- Claims often require linking multiple facts across passages (e.g., birthplace and birth date, co-starring relationships, production details).  
- Beware of nuanced wording such as "not both," "earlier than," or "partly" which may require verifying multiple conditions.

Use this approach consistently to produce accurate, evidence-grounded summaries assessing the veracity of the claim with respect to the provided passages.

Iteration 7: Proposed new text for program.create_query_hop3.predict: You will receive inputs containing three fields: 
- `claim`: a factual or composite statement referencing entities, events, or attributes.
- `summary_1` and `summary_2`: two human-generated summaries evaluating the claim against one or more provided passages. Each summary assesses whether the claim is supported, partially supported, not supported, or not verifiable based on the evidence in those passages, sometimes commenting on what evidence is present or missing.

Your task is to produce the field `query`, which must be a clear, precise, and targeted search query or set of searches that would help verify or falsify all components of the claim, with an emphasis on the unsupported or ambiguous parts highlighted in the summaries.

To generate a high-quality `query`, follow these principles:

1. **Decompose the claim into distinct factual assertions** — especially those identified as unsupported, partially supported, or unverified by the summaries.

2. **Identify the specific missing or uncertain facts** based on summary_1 and summary_2. This may include:
   - Verifying casting, production, or credited roles (e.g., actors, choreographers, musicians)
   - Confirming biographical details (e.g., nationality, birth year, ancestry)
   - Establishing company ownership, production credits, or headquarters location
   - Clarifying historical event details (dates, participants, roles)
   - Checking valuation or dedication claims
   - Confirming performance or association of named groups or individuals

3. **Include domain-specific keywords and identifiers** that help pinpoint the relevant information in a search context, such as:
   - Names of persons, productions, companies, or works
   - Roles or titles (e.g., choreographer, signer, actor playing a role)
   - Dates or years relevant to events or productions
   - Official identifiers like IATA/ICAO codes for airports when applicable
   - Alternate or variant spellings of names/terms when relevant
   - Keywords related to the nature of the claim (e.g., "ownership", "stake", "birth year", "film debut", "headquartered in")

4. **Use logical structuring:**
   - If helpful, format the query as numbered bullet points or grouped search phrases to clearly separate distinct verification needs.
   - Suggest specific authoritative sources or domains when possible (e.g., IMDb, Wikipedia, official company websites, academic or government domains `.edu` or `.gov`, Discogs for music credits).

5. **Aim for queries that a researcher or fact-checker could input directly into search engines or specialized databases to retrieve authoritative evidence that directly supports or refutes each component of the claim.**

6. **Do not assume availability of the underlying passages; focus solely on what is missing or ambiguous as indicated by the summaries.**

7. **The reasoning step (often present in examples) is not to be included in your output — only produce the final `query` field as described above.**

Example (not to be included, for understanding only):

For a claim about an actor's role and a company's shareholder stake, if summaries say the passages support the actor's role but do not confirm shareholder ownership, your query should include:
- Confirming the actor's credited role in the production.
- Locating updated, reliable sources on the company's ownership structure, including potential shareholders or equity stakes.

This systematic, detailed approach ensures comprehensive, precise, and actionable queries for fact verification.

Iteration 9: Proposed new text for program.summarize2.predict: Given three fields:  
- `claim`: a factual assertion or statement to be verified, often combining multiple components or references, sometimes including comparisons or complex relationships;  
- `context`: a brief human-generated summary or judgment about the claim's veracity based on given passages, sometimes highlighting nuances like partial truth or need for additional sources;  
- `passages`: a list of text excerpts providing factual data relevant to the claim; these may include detailed descriptions, lists, biographies, filmographies, award details, dates, and other domain-specific facts.

Your task is to produce the `summary` field, which is a concise, well-reasoned, evidence-based verification of the claim based strictly on the information found in the provided passages.

When producing the `summary`, adhere to the following detailed guidance:

1. **Core Function**: Evaluate the claim against the evidence in the provided passages alone. Determine if the claim is Supported, Partly Supported/Partially True, Not Supported, or Not Enough Information / Insufficient Information.

2. **Use Explicit Passage Evidence**:  
   - Reference relevant facts, including names, dates, roles, film titles, awards, organizational affiliations, locations, and other directly stated data.  
   - Do not infer beyond what the passages explicitly support. If links or relationships are missing in the passages, highlight this.

3. **Address Multi-part Claims**:  
   - If the claim includes multiple assertions, evaluate each aspect separately.  
   - The summary should reflect which parts are supported and which are not, making clear distinctions.

4. **Context Utilization**:  
   - Consider the provided `context` field that frames the claim’s truthfulness in light of the passages.  
   - Use context to understand overall judgment or partial truths, but base your summary’s factual backing solely on the passages.

5. **Maintain Factual Accuracy and Clarity**:  
   - Correct any inaccuracies or misstatements found in the claim when contradicted by passages.  
   - If the claim contains errors (e.g. wrong film title, incorrect numeric facts), state this clearly.

6. **Niche / Domain Specific Knowledge Embedded in Passages**:  
   Be aware that passages may contain:  
   - Film titles, directors, actors, and their roles or biographies, including linking actors to roles or films.  
   - Details on awards (nominations, ceremonies).  
   - Information about specific organizations, institutions (universities, workshops, writing programs), including founding dates and notable members.  
   - Information about airlines, aircraft, ownership stakes, and industry alliances.  
   - Details of geographic locations and their relationships (e.g., metro regions, airports, and their rankings).  
   - Languages and indigenous groups, especially uncontacted peoples.  
   - Magazine titles, their target demographics, mergers, and publication details.  
   - Product ownership chains, headquarters locations, and manufacturing details.  

Use these domain elements precisely as provided without adding outside knowledge.

7. **Avoid Speculation or External Knowledge**:  
   - If crucial information is not provided in the passages, state clearly that the evidence is insufficient or no information is available.  
   - Avoid guessing or using knowledge not present in the passages.

8. **Output Style**:  
   - The summary should be a brief, clear, and factual paragraph or two that directly states the level of support and briefly explains why, citing evidence or lack thereof as appropriate.  
   - Use clear terms like “Supported”, “Not supported”, “Partially supported”, or “Insufficient information” based on your reasoning.

9. **Reasoning Field**:  
   If producing a reasoning field (not always required), detail your step-by-step inference process, linking claims to passages and elucidating missing links where the claim cannot be confirmed.

In essence: **produce a factual, passage-grounded verification summary of the claim’s truthfulness, explicitly referencing or noting the contents of the given passages.**

Iteration 15: Proposed new text for program.summarize2.predict: Given input fields `claim`, `context`, and `passages`, your task is to produce the output field `summary` that clearly verifies, refutes, partially supports, or states there is insufficient evidence for the claim based solely on the passages provided.

Detailed instructions:

1. **Understand the claim carefully.** Identify all components, especially if the claim contains multiple parts or comparisons.

2. **Analyze the passages carefully.** Extract all relevant factual information related to the entities, dates, events, and relationships mentioned in the claim. Look for:  
   - Explicit factual statements supporting or contradicting the claim.  
   - Dates (birth, death, event years, publication years).  
   - Named entities such as people, places, organizations, works (films, books, music), roles, or titles.  
   - Key attributes related to those entities: e.g., professions, nationalities, relationships, roles played in productions, locations, historical data, etc.

3. **Use the `context` field as guidance** on the degree of evidence and key points already identified. This helps orient whether the claim is fully supported, refuted, partially supported, or if evidence is missing.

4. **Reason about the claim in light of the evidence:**  
   - Fully supported: All components of the claim have explicit supporting evidence in the passages.  
   - Refuted: The passages contradict the claim clearly.  
   - Partially Supported: Some parts of the claim are supported, but others are not addressed or contradicted.  
   - Insufficient evidence: The passages lack enough evidence to verify or refute the claim.

5. **Compare facts carefully:**  
   - When the claim compares two or more people, events, or entities (e.g., dates, careers, nationalities), be certain to find supporting info for each and perform the comparison.  
   - If data for one element is missing, note that verification is impossible.

6. **Be precise and explicit in your summary.** Include:  
   - Which parts are supported, which are refuted or not supported.  
   - What evidence was found or missing (e.g., dates, places, roles).  
   - If relevant, report contradictions.

7. **Examples of domain knowledge incorporated:**  
   - Birth and death dates and places to establish timeline comparisons.  
   - Definitions of roles and professions, e.g., "bass" = lowest vocal range.  
   - Film credits, roles in productions, nationality of people and films, production years.  
   - Botanical family names and their alphabetical order.  
   - Historical context about authorship and historical timelines.  
   - Understanding that "fictional character" vs. "actor" distinctions matter.  
   - Use of opera, film, book titles, and knowledge of collaborations.  
   - Awareness of the accuracy levels: some evidence can be partial or disproved by date mismatches.

8. **Avoid speculation or relying on info not present in the passages.** The summary must be grounded solely in supplied passages and context.

9. **If the claim refers to multiple details, address each part accordingly.** For example, if there are two or more comparisons or factual elements, handle all cleanly in the summary.

---

In summary, your output `summary` should be a concise, factual, precise, and comprehensive assessment of the claim’s veracity given the passages and context. It should explain the reasoning behind your conclusion with reference to the evidence or lack thereof.

Iteration 17: Proposed new text for program.summarize2.predict: You are given three fields as inputs:  
- `claim`: a statement or assertion that may contain multiple factual components about people, places, events, works (e.g., films, albums, shows), or entities along with specific attributes or relationships.  
- `context`: a high-level assessment of whether the claim is supported, partially supported, or unsupported based on the evidence provided, often with reasoning details about what parts are supported and what parts are not. This is for reference but the assistant should independently verify.  
- `passages`: a list of textual evidence passages relevant to the claim, containing factual information that may confirm, partially confirm, or fail to confirm the claim.

Your task is to produce a concise `summary` that assesses the claim’s veracity with respect to the supplied passages. The `summary` must:  

1. Analyze individual elements of the claim (which may have multiple parts or can be ambiguous) by checking them against the provided passages.  
2. Distinguish between:  
   - Fully supported elements: explicitly stated or clearly inferred facts from the passages directly matching the claim’s details.  
   - Partially supported elements: elements for which there is indirect, implied, or ambiguous evidence, or only some claim components are supported.  
   - Unsupported or unverifiable elements: claim facets missing from all passages or contradicted by the evidence.  
3. Identify uncertainty or ambiguity in claims when applicable, explaining which interpretation is supported or unsupported.  
4. If the claim compares or relates multiple entities (e.g., comparisons of quantities, timelines, or attributes), confirm the presence of relevant comparative data before affirming.  
5. Where possible, cite supporting or contradicting facts succinctly to inform the summary reasoning without restating the full passages.  
6. Reflect the evidence-based conclusion in a balanced way, marking the claim as "Supported," "Partially supported," or "Not supported / Cannot verify" accordingly.

Additional domain-specific notes:  
- The subject matter can include, but is not limited to, biographies, films and roles, music albums and artists, educational institutions and their characteristics, video games and software, historical figures and familial relationships, and other named entities.  
- Roles such as “director,” “screenwriter,” “actor,” “chancellor,” and “singer” require precise verification from the passages.  
- Dates, locations, and numeric details (population, number of albums, release years) must be explicitly supported or else treated as unverifiable.  
- When the claim involves awards, nominations, or recognitions, ensure the exact award/nominator and title matches the passages; approximate or related awards are not sufficient for full support.  
- Carefully assess claims involving ambiguous phrasing or multiple possible readings; clarify which interpretations the evidence backs.  
- If critical details about comparative claims (e.g., counts, durations) are missing from the evidence, the claim should be treated as unverified regarding that part.

Approach:  
- Carefully parse the claim to isolate each factual assertion.  
- Cross-check each assertion systematically against the passages.  
- Note the presence or absence of direct, indirect, or contradicting evidence for each.  
- Formulate a concise reasoning summary that transparently conveys which parts are supported and which are not, referencing key named entities or passages as needed.  
- Conclude with an overall judgment reflecting the collective support level of the claim.

Output:  
Provide only the concise summary, stating the support level and the rationale as outlined, without extraneous commentary.

Iteration 19: Proposed new text for program.create_query_hop3.predict: You are given three fields:  
- `claim`: A factual statement or assertion possibly involving multiple entities, dates, attributes, and relationships.  
- `summary_1` and `summary_2`: Two text summaries that analyze or evaluate the `claim` based on a set of provided source passages (not shown here). These summaries express whether the claim is supported, partially supported, not supported, or if information is insufficient, and often cite specific passages or facts.

Your task is to produce the field:  
- `query`: a natural language search query or set of queries designed to retrieve additional authoritative information needed to verify, confirm, or refute the claim fully and accurately.

**Detailed Task Description:**  
1. **Understand the claim thoroughly:**  
   - Break down the claim into its components, including named entities (people, places, works, organizations), relationships, dates, and any numeric or categorical assertions.  
   - Identify any compound claims or multiple sub-claims.

2. **Compare and analyze `summary_1` and `summary_2`:**  
   - Note agreements and disagreements in the summaries about the claim's truthfulness or evidential support.  
   - Identify what evidence or lack thereof each summary highlights from the source passages, including inconsistencies, missing information, or contradictions.

3. **Identify factual or evidentiary gaps:**  
   - Determine what critical information is missing or uncertain that prevents a definitive truth judgment on the claim. This may be:  
     - Confirming specific factual details (dates, works, persons’ roles, nationalities, locations).  
     - Identifying relationships or links between entities (e.g., did person X star in film Y, was candy Z produced by company A headquartered in state B).  
     - Verifying numeric facts or quantitative comparisons.  
   - Pinpoint any ambiguous or vague parts of the claim needing clarification or external verification.

4. **Formulate comprehensive, targeted search queries:**  
   - Queries should be explicit, clear, and focused on retrieving the missing or doubtful information. Typical query elements include:  
     - Named entities (full names, alternative spellings).  
     - Key attributes or titles (roles, jobs, birthplaces, awards).  
     - Relationships (e.g., "X directed Y", "Z starred in Y", "Location of X", "Headquarters of Company A").  
     - Dates and time frames to narrow results.  
     - Often include phrasing or keywords from the claim to maximize relevancy.  
     - Multiple queries or bullet points when more than one distinct information piece is required.  
   - Include instructions to seek authoritative sources (e.g., IMDb, Wikipedia, taxonomic databases, census data, etc.) based on the domain.

5. **Incorporate reasoning as needed:**  
   - When appropriate, briefly explain the reasoning behind the search focus derived from the summaries and claim dissection.  
   - Clarify which part(s) of the claim remain unresolved or contradictory and how the query addresses them.

6. **Avoid simple restatements of the claim:**  
   - Queries should not merely echo the claim but instead target the factual evidence gaps or verification points highlighted by the summaries and your reasoning.

7. **Domain-specific and niche considerations observed from examples:**  
   - Film/TV domain: check director credits, cast lists, associations between actors and films, awards won, exact years of releases or events.  
   - Literature: authorship, nationality, prize awards, relationships between works and authors.  
   - Biology/Botany: species names, taxonomic classifications, native range, distinctions between genus and species.  
   - Corporate/business: corporate ownership, headquarters locations, brand production and marketing relationships.  
   - Historical/geographical: verify dates of establishment, death years, population data, locations of landmarks or institutions.  
   - Music: collaborations, discography, roles within bands or projects.  
   - Video games: game content details, references to brands/products within games.  

8. **If the claim is fully supported or refuted by the summaries and passages, the query output can reflect that with a keyword or a brief confirmation, such as "SUPPORTED" or a simple direct query encapsulating the verified fact.**

**Summary:**  
Given the `claim`, `summary_1`, and `summary_2`, produce a precise search query or queries that target the exact missing factual evidence needed to confirm or deny the claim. Leverage differences or confirmations in the summaries, domain knowledge illustrated in examples, and extract specific facts to verify. Provide multi-faceted queries when necessary. Use concise yet comprehensive phrasing suitable for querying external authoritative sources or databases to resolve all claim ambiguities.

Iteration 21: Proposed new text for program.create_query_hop2.predict: Task Description:

You will be provided with inputs containing two fields:

- `claim`: A factual statement or set of factual assertions, possibly containing multiple discrete facts and relationships regarding people, events, works, organizations, places, or other entities.
- `summary_1`: An evaluation or summary assessing the claim, indicating which parts are supported, unsupported, contradicted, or partially supported by given evidence or passages.

Your objective is to produce a comprehensive and precise `query` field consisting of focused search queries and information requests. These queries are designed to efficiently retrieve authoritative, verifiable evidence that conclusively supports, refutes, or clarifies each factual element or relationship in the claim that is not fully supported per `summary_1`.

---

Detailed Instruction for Query Generation:

1. **Decompose the Claim into Discrete Factual Components:**
   - Identify every fact, whether a single assertion or a relationship (e.g., person-role, film-director, event-date, organizational affiliation).
   - Recognize when the claim involves multiple components that require individual verification.
   - Note which of these components are supported or unsupported according to `summary_1`.

2. **Domain-Specific Considerations and Terminology:**
   - Detect domain and relevant entities: e.g., sports players, film titles, historical figures, geographic locations, scientific terms, musical artists, companies, literary works.
   - Extract and incorporate distinctive domain-specific terms (e.g., game engines, tournament names, actor roles, legal entity names, release years, linguistic terms, biological classifications).
   - Include alternate names, common misspellings, or closely related entities when ambiguity or conflation is possible.

3. **Design Queries to Target Authoritative Sources:**
   - For biographies, find official or reputable profiles, e.g., university pages, national libraries, music databases, or sports statistics repositories.
   - For film and entertainment, rely on IMDb, official studio pages, industry publications, recognized encyclopedias, and press coverage.
   - For historical and geographic data, prioritize government archives, historical societies, scholarly publications, or official institutional websites.
   - For scientific, linguistic, or botanical data, use academic databases, taxonomic registries, linguistic corpora, or encyclopedias.
   - When dealing with official corporate or organisational data, use government filings, SEC or equivalent, corporate websites, and trusted business databases.

4. **Focus Queries on Unverified or Partially Verified Elements:**
   - For factual elements marked as unsupported or lacking information, queries should explicitly seek evidence addressing those gaps.
   - For partially supported claims, queries should clarify ambiguous details (dates, names, relationships).
   - When misinformation, confusion, or conflation is evident, design queries to disambiguate entities and separate facts.

5. **Explicit Verification of Relationships and Roles:**
   - Formulate queries to confirm relationships such as "X co-starred with Y," "Person A directed film B," or "Company C acquired entity D in year E."
   - For chronological claims, queries should seek dates of birth, formation, release, or event occurrence from reliable sources.
   - For claims involving comparisons (e.g., "plays more instruments than"), queries should be designed to produce authoritative lists or verified credits for all compared parties.

6. **Use Search Strategies to Enhance Precision and Coverage:**
   - Employ exact phrase quotes to narrow down to relevant facts.
   - Use logical operators (AND, OR) and site restrictions (e.g., site:imdb.com, site:sec.gov, site:britannica.com) to target specific sources.
   - Include multiple queries separately numbered or bulleted to explicitly separate factual elements.
   - If relevant, query variations or synonyms for names/terms to cover alternate spellings or closely related entries.

7. **Request Direct Citations or Textual Excerpts Where Possible:**
   - Specify retrieval of exact sentences, quotes, or excerpt lines from authoritative sources to directly support or refute each fact.
   - For biographical or historical claims, seek entries containing explicit dates, titles, or descriptions.
   - For entertainment claims, request official cast/crew credit lines or production company listings.
   - For scientific or linguistic claims, request classification entries or original paper citations.

8. **Instructions to Handle Ambiguous or Conflicting Information:**
   - If authoritative sources differ, request summary of each with corresponding citations.
   - In cases where an entity appears in multiple identities (e.g., multiple artists with same name), include queries to disambiguate identities with biographical references.
   - For place or organizational names with multiple possible references, include disambiguation queries specifying state, country, or context.

---

Overall, your `query` output will be a structured, well-organized set of clear, targeted search instructions or question prompts that enable a human or automated researcher to retrieve the most reliable, relevant, and precise information needed to definitively verify every factual component and relationship in the `claim`, especially focusing on components not yet verified or supported in `summary_1`.

Adopt a consistent analytical approach: Identify, classify, isolate unsupported facts, apply domain knowledge to formulate context-aware queries, direct queries toward highest-authority sources, and clearly delineate each search to cover all aspects thoroughly.

Iteration 27: Proposed new text for create_query_hop2.predict: You are assisting with a multi-hop fact verification or fact-finding task where the goal is to generate a precise, targeted retrieval query for the second retrieval hop. The input each time consists of:

1. A complex factual claim that often involves multiple entities, relationships, or events.
2. A set of titles or named entities retrieved in the first retrieval hop—these typically include entities directly or indirectly relevant to the claim.

Your task is to generate the **second retrieval query** to gather information that will confirm or refute the claim, by building on the output of the first retrieval. The goal is to identify supporting facts or verify connections involved in the claim that were not fully resolved in the first hop.

Key details and guidelines to consider:

- Use the claim and hop-1 titles to identify the missing pieces of information or unresolved relationships needed to verify the claim.
- Target your queries specifically to extract those missing facts. Focus on combining entities, events, titles, or keywords that capture the crucial aspects of the claim for the second retrieval.
- Queries should be precise but flexible enough to retrieve relevant documents or passages, often including:
    - Named entities (persons, works, organizations, locations, etc.) from claim and hop-1 results.
    - Keywords related to roles, relationships, events, dates, biographical details, production credits, or geographical facts.
    - Phrases that indicate relationships or attributes, such as "director", "cast", "headquartered", "biography", "release year", "producer", "formation date", "nationality", "affiliations", "brand value", "ownership", etc.
- Use logical operators or grouped entity lists (e.g., OR) when several candidates must be disambiguated or checked simultaneously.
- When dates/times, rankings, or quantitative data are part of the claim, queries should explicitly seek this information.
- Consider synonyms and variations in wording (e.g., "director" vs. "director credit", "production company" vs. "studio").
- Avoid queries that are too broad/vague or too narrowly restrictive so that relevant supporting documents are not retrieved.
- The queries need not be full natural-language questions; they can be keyword-rich strings combining relevant terms and entities.

Examples of useful strategies illustrated:

- If the claim combines multiple facts about a subject (e.g., a person’s educational background combined with institutional data), queries should focus on the missing link (e.g., the person's degree-granting institution).
- When verifying relations among multiple entities (e.g., a film and its prequel or a band member and membership chronology), incorporate both entities and relationship descriptors.
- When verifying numerical or timeframe claims (dates, rankings, sales numbers), explicitly seek sources that mention these figures tied to the entities.
- When several possible candidates emerge from hop 1, include them as OR alternatives to compare their associations with the claim.
- When verifying a title’s association with creators, release years, or production studios, include those specific attributes.
- Use the hop-1 results as focal points or anchors, referencing key terms from those titles or entities to refine queries.

In summary, your output must be a single, clear, and focused query string poised to lead to retrieval of the critical corroborating facts required for subsequent claim validation, rooted in the interplay of the claim’s content and the hop-1 retrieval output.

Iteration 28: Proposed new text for program.summarize2.predict: Given three fields:  
- `claim`: a factual assertion or statement to be verified, often combining multiple components or references, sometimes including comparisons or complex relationships;  
- `context`: a brief human-generated summary or judgment about the claim's veracity based on given passages, sometimes highlighting nuances like partial truth or need for additional sources;  
- `passages`: a list of text excerpts providing factual data relevant to the claim; these may include detailed descriptions, lists, biographies, filmographies, award details, dates, and other domain-specific facts.

Your task is to produce the `summary` field, which is a concise, well-reasoned, evidence-based verification of the claim based strictly on the information found in the provided passages.

When producing the `summary`, adhere to the following detailed guidance:

1. **Core Function**: Evaluate the claim against the evidence in the provided passages alone. Determine if the claim is Supported, Partly Supported/Partially True, Not Supported, or Not Enough Information / Insufficient Information.

2. **Use Explicit Passage Evidence**:  
   - Reference relevant facts, including names, dates, roles, film titles, awards, organizational affiliations, locations, and other directly stated data.  
   - Do not infer beyond what the passages explicitly support. If links or relationships are missing in the passages, highlight this.

3. **Address Multi-part Claims**:  
   - If the claim includes multiple assertions, evaluate each aspect separately.  
   - The summary should reflect which parts are supported and which are not, making clear distinctions.

4. **Context Utilization**:  
   - Consider the provided `context` field that frames the claim’s truthfulness in light of the passages.  
   - Use context to understand overall judgment or partial truths, but base your summary’s factual backing solely on the passages.

5. **Maintain Factual Accuracy and Clarity**:  
   - Correct any inaccuracies or misstatements found in the claim when contradicted by passages.  
   - If the claim contains errors (e.g. wrong film title, incorrect numeric facts), state this clearly.

6. **Niche / Domain Specific Knowledge Embedded in Passages**:  
   Be aware that passages may contain:  
   - Film titles, directors, actors, and their roles or biographies, including linking actors to roles or films.  
   - Details on awards (nominations, ceremonies).  
   - Information about specific organizations, institutions (universities, workshops, writing programs), including founding dates and notable members.  
   - Information about airlines, aircraft, ownership stakes, and industry alliances.  
   - Details of geographic locations and their relationships (e.g., metro regions, airports, and their rankings).  
   - Languages and indigenous groups, especially uncontacted peoples.  
   - Magazine titles, their target demographics, mergers, and publication details.  
   - Product ownership chains, headquarters locations, and manufacturing details.  

Use these domain elements precisely as provided without adding outside knowledge.

7. **Avoid Speculation or External Knowledge**:  
   - If crucial information is not provided in the passages, state clearly that the evidence is insufficient or no information is available.  
   - Avoid guessing or using knowledge not present in the passages.

8. **Output Style**:  
   - The summary should be a brief, clear, and factual paragraph or two that directly states the level of support and briefly explains why, citing evidence or lack thereof as appropriate.  
   - Use clear terms like “Supported”, “Not supported”, “Partially supported”, or “Insufficient information” based on your reasoning.

9. **Reasoning Field**:  
   If producing a reasoning field (not always required), detail your step-by-step inference process, linking claims to passages and elucidating missing links where the claim cannot be confirmed.

In essence: **produce a factual, passage-grounded verification summary of the claim’s truthfulness, explicitly referencing or noting the contents of the given passages.**

Iteration 29: Proposed new text for program.create_query_hop3.predict: You will be given three text fields as input: `claim`, `summary_1`, and `summary_2`.

- The `claim` field contains a factual or composite statement that may combine multiple assertions about people, works, places, or events.
- The `summary_1` and `summary_2` fields provide independent assessments of the claim's veracity based on supplied evidence passages. They note which elements of the claim are supported, partially supported, unsupported, contradicted, or unverifiable.

Your task is to generate a `query` field designed to help verify or falsify the claim, considering both summaries. The query should be a focused, well-structured set of search instructions or specific questions that will gather authoritative evidence to:

1. Confirm or refute each claim element, especially those marked as unsupported, unverifiable, or contradicted.
2. Fill gaps where the provided passages do not supply enough evidence.
3. Clarify ambiguous or conflated information, including identification of persons, works, roles, dates, or relationships.
4. Explicitly request source citations or factual data where helpful.
5. Target domain-specific entities or facts mentioned in the claim (e.g., film titles, persons’ real names, organizational roles, release years, historical relationships, music videos, sports events, etc.).
6. Suggest queries that can retrieve structured factual information (e.g., filmography lists, biographical data, official award records, performance credits).

Additional guidance:

- When the claim is partially supported or has conflicting elements, the query should be comprehensive yet precise to isolate each aspect that needs verification.
- If the summaries contradict, you should reconcile them in your reasoning and cover verification of the disputed elements in the query.
- The queries should be formulated as explicit search strings, targeted keywords, or question formats that can be directly used in a search engine or research database. Use quotes for exact phrases or names when applicable.
- Where multiple steps or sub-claims exist, organize the query as numbered or bulleted items for clarity.
- Avoid vague or overly broad queries; specificity is crucial to retrieve relevant and authoritative sources.
- Acknowledge negative claim checks (e.g., a person not involved in an event, or an absence of relation) by including searches that can confirm or refute such nonoccurrences.
- Include recognized domain-specific terminology, variant spellings, or well-known aliases to improve search effectiveness (e.g., "aka," "stage name," "director," "producer," "filmography," "cast list," "award nominations," "tournament draws," "commercial brand ownership").
- Aim to facilitate the collection of concrete evidence such as release dates, biographical details, official credits, and credible citations.

Your output should be structured as follows:

### reasoning  
A concise explanation synthesizing the two summaries, highlighting supported, unsupported, contradictory, or missing information, and identifying precisely what needs further verification.

### query  
A well-structured list of precise search queries or questions that, when researched, would verify or refute the claim’s components.

---

Example (conceptual):

Given claim:  
"Actor X starred in Film Y and also directed Film Z before 2010."

If summaries say Film Y acting credit is supported, but no evidence for Film Z directing or date, your reasoning would highlight that and your query would request confirming Film Z’s director and release date with search strings like:

- "Film Z director"  
- "Film Z release date"  
- "Actor X filmography"  

---

This task requires:

- Careful comparison of claim against given summaries.  
- Decomposition of complex claims into factual elements.  
- Identification of missing or conflicting facts in supporting evidence.  
- Creation of targeted search queries suited for verifying specialized factual information spanning domains like film, music, history, sport, or corporate data.  

Remember, your role is to bridge the gap between partial evidence and full claim verification by crafting direct and domain-aware queries.

Iteration 31: Proposed new text for create_query_hop3.predict: You are given:  
- A factual claim that needs to be verified by consulting knowledge sources in multiple retrieval hops.  
- Two sets of titles returned from prior retrieval hops (hop 1 and hop 2) that relate to entities, works, people, or concepts mentioned in the claim.  

Your task:  
Generate a precise, focused third retrieval query that will most effectively retrieve supporting facts to verify or refute the claim. This query should be constructed based on the claim itself and the titles retrieved in hops 1 and 2.

Instructions and domain-specific details:  
1. **Understand the claim thoroughly**: Identify the key entities, relationships, events, attributes, or comparisons that must be verified. Claims often involve dates (birth, release, award years), biographical data, relationships (family, collaborators, group membership), production details, attribution of works, geographic or nationality information, or ranking and counts (e.g., number of albums, awards).  

2. **Analyze retrieved titles from hops 1 and 2**: These titles represent potential sources or entities related to claim components. Titles may be people, works (films, albums, books), organizations, concepts, places, or events. Use these as anchors or keywords in the next query to seek specific factual statements.  

3. **Target the “missing link” or verification fact**: The next query should attempt to surface explicit information not yet confirmed, e.g., birth dates of two people to compare order, production or filming locations for two films, detailed discography numbers, award wins by individuals or groups, personnel or membership lists, or biographical details connecting people and places.  

4. **Construct the query as an effective retrieval phrase**:  
   - Include key entity names from hops 1 and 2, especially those directly relevant to the claim’s main fact(s).  
   - Add relevant keywords that fetch the fact type needed (e.g., “date of birth”, “director”, “filming locations”, “awards won”, “band members”, “discography”, “merger”, “target audience”, “dedication”, “nationality”, “stake”, “ownership”, “birthplace”).  
   - Use boolean operators (AND, OR), quotation marks for exact phrases, parentheses for grouping, and synonyms or alternate names/terms where appropriate to maximize recall and precision. Include factual specifics such as years, percentages, or comparative terms if they appear in the claim.  
   - When a clarifying fact depends on more than one entity or concept, combine them in the query logically to find sources that link multiple components.  

5. **Multifaceted claims**: If the claim includes multiple points (e.g., magazine origin, album release, ranking), combine query elements so the retrieval can hit multiple supporting facts in a single search, improving efficiency.  

6. **Use domain-specific and contextual knowledge learned from examples**:  
   - Film claims commonly require director, lead actor, filming location, award, or production info.  
   - Music claims often need album release dates, dedication details, discography counts, or personnel roles.  
   - Biography claims center on birth dates, relationships, nationalities, or published works.  
   - Organization or publication claims focus on mergers, demographics, ownership, or historical facts.  

7. **Avoid vague or ambiguous queries** that lack connection to prior hops or do not specify needed fact types clearly. Avoid overly generic terms without entity anchors, as these reduce precision.  

8. **Expected output**: Provide the succinct next retrieval query as a single string containing relevant entity names, keywords, and Boolean logic, ready for input to a retrieval system.

By following these instructions, your query will help fetch authoritative and precise supporting facts critical for claim verification in the subsequent retrieval hop.
